v
Abstract
Maximal curves, that is curves deпђБned over a пђБnite пђБeld which attain
the upper Hasse-Weil bound are of interest in coding theory as they give
rise to excellent AG codes. In this project we study such curves and their
relationship to Hermitian varieties, which is stated by the Natural Embedding
theorem. To study maximal curves in this fashion we begin by deпђБning and
investigating the properties of Hermitian varieties before tackling the Natural
Embedding theorem itself. One direction of the Natural Embedding theorem
will be proved in full outside of a few details.
Finally we take a look at
one implication of the Natural embedding theorem, which gives a description
of maximal curves of Frobenius dimension 3.
This description пђБrst given
by Fanali & Giulietti is improved upon in this project which in turn allows
us to exhaustively search through all the candidates for maximal curves of
Frobenius dimension 3 over рЭФљ16.

CHAPTER 0
Introduction
In this project we will study algebraic curves over пђБnite пђБelds and in particular
we will study the maximal curves, which are those curves that have the maximal
number of rational places. By the maximal number of rational places we mean
that the number of rational places attains the upper Hasse-Weil bound, that is for
a curve рЭРґover our пђБnite пђБeld рЭФљрЭСЮ2 we have
|рЭРґ(рЭФљрЭСЮ2)| = рЭСЮ2 + 1 + 2вДКрЭСЮ,
where вДКis the genus of рЭРґ. Our motivation for studying these curves is that they
give rise to excellent algebraic geometry (AG) codes.
In the пђБeld of coding theory we usually want to balance three quantities: The
length of a code рЭСЫ, the dimension of a code рЭСШand the minimum distance of a code
рЭСС. The length of the code dictates how long a codeword is, so for instance a code of
length 128 over рЭФљ2 consists of 128 bits. The dimension of a code dictates how much
information a codeword conveys, so for instance if a code of length 128 over рЭФљ2
has dimension 80, then each codeword conveys 80 bits of information. Finally, the
minimum distance gives a measure of how many errors one can hope to correct. As
a rule of thumb we can hope to correct about half as many errors as the minimum
distance (вМК(рЭССвИТ1)/2вМЛto be precise). To get a good code we want рЭСШand рЭССto be
large, while the rate рЭСШ/рЭСЫis small. However, there is always a tradeoпђАbetween the
three quantities as they are intimately related by the Singleton bound.
Theorem 0.0.1 (Singleton bound). For a (linear) code рЭТЮits parameters рЭСЫ, рЭСШ, рЭСС
satisfy
рЭССвЙ§рЭСЫвИТрЭСШ+ 1.
In the case of AG codes the situation looks a little diпђАerent and we will usually
do a little worse:
Theorem 0.0.2 (AG code parameters). Under some assumptions an AG code рЭТЮ
has parameters рЭСЫ, рЭСШ, рЭССsatisfying
рЭСС= рЭСЫвИТрЭСШ+ 1 вИТвДК,
where вДКis the genus of the function пђБeld giving rise to the code.
==PAGE:12==
2
CHAPTER 0.
INTRODUCTION
The best way to get an excellent code despite the limitations imposed by the
above theorems is to have long codes. To illustrate the advantage of longer codes
let us look at a simple comparison. Consider a channel, where the probability of a
symbol being changed is 5%. A code of length 10 and dimension 6 (a rate of 60%)
with the maximal minimum distance of 5 can correct 2 errors, which means that
1.15% percent of the time our error-correcting code fails to decode the codeword
correctly. On the other hand a code of length 100 with a dimension of 60 (i.e. the
same rate) and minimum distance of 41 can correct 20 errors, which results in the
probability of failure being a measly 0.00000208%.
Now the length of an AG code is limited precisely by the number of rational
places of the underlying function пђБeld, so if we can пђБnd a curve with many rational
places, then it can be used to construct a good AG code, hence the desire to study
maximal curves.
In this project we will work our way towards the Natural Embedding theorem,
which tells us of an intimate relationship between maximal curves and Hermitian
varieties. More precisely it tells us that any maximal curve has an embedding in a
Hermitian variety (as a smooth curve of degree рЭСЮ+ 1). In fact the converse is also
true, however we will only show the пђБrst implication. Using this knowledge we
can say something about the structure of maximal curves of Frobenius dimension
3, which will help us пђБnd maximal curves over рЭФљрЭСЮ2 for рЭСЮ= 4.
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 1
Preliminaries
In this chapter we lay the ground work for the project by stating the basic deпђБ-
nitions and properties that we will need in our study of maximal curves. For the
sake of brevity most proofs have been omitted, but the interested reader can look
in [1], [11] and [13], which the chapter is loosely based upon.
In section 1 we state some basic deпђБnitions concerning пђБelds and пђБeld extensions
and some basic facts concerning polynomials. In section 2 we deпђБne the notion of
an algebraic variety and the maps between such varieties. In section 3 we look at
the special case of an algebraic curve or more generally an algebraic function пђБeld
and develop the main results of this theory. In section 4 we deпђБne Riemann-Roch
spaces, which are extremely important in the study of algebraic function пђБelds. In
section 5 we tie back some of the results of section 4 to maps between algebraic
curves. Finally in section 6 we state the contents of the Hasse Weil theorem, which
will then lead to the notion of a maximal algebraic function пђБeld.
1.1
Polynomials and пђБeld extensions
We begin with some deпђБnitions concerning пђБeld extensions.
DeпђБnition 1.1.1. Let рЭРєвКЗрЭРЊbe a пђБeld extension.

2.2
The Hermitian varieties
With thorough knowledge of the structure of Hermitian matrices we can now move

DeпђБnition 2.2.1. Let рЭРЊ= рЭФљрЭСЮ2 and рЭРїbe a Hermitian matrix of order рЭСБ+ 2 for
рЭСБвЙ•0 and rank рЭСЯ. Then a Hermitian variety of dimension рЭСБand rank рЭСЯis the

An element рЭС•вИИрЭСЙрЭСБis called a point on the Hermitian variety. If the Hermitian
matrix in question has full rank, we call the corresponding Hermitian variety
nondegenerate and degenerate otherwise.
Remark 2.2.2. Note that the above deпђБnition makes sense, since if рЭС•, рЭС¶вИИрЭФЄрЭСБ+2 are
such that рЭС•вИЉрЭС¶, i.e. рЭС¶= рЭЫЉрЭС•for some рЭЫЉвИИрЭФљрЭСЮ2 вІµ{0} and рЭС•вИЧрЭРїрЭС•= 0, then also

Moreover, suppose рЭРї, рЭРЇare equivalent so that рЭРЇ= рЭРівИЧрЭРїрЭРіfor some nonsingular
square matrix рЭРі. If рЭС•is a point of the Hermitian variety corresponding to рЭРї,

point of the Hermitian variety corresponding to рЭРЇ. Hence, any two equivalent
Hermitian matrices give rise to the same Hermitian variety up to a linear change
of coordinates.

CHAPTER 3
The Natural Embedding Theorem
We will now begin our study of the Natural Embedding Theorem, which states
that any maximal curve has an embedding in a Hermitian variety as a nonsingular
curve of degree рЭСЮ. Throughout this chapter рЭРґwill be our nonsingular maximal
projective curve deпђБned over рЭФљрЭСЮ2. That is the number of рЭФљрЭСЮ2-rational points of рЭРґ
attains the upper Hasse-Weil bound, namely
|рЭРґ(рЭФљрЭСЮ2)| = рЭСЮ2 + 1 + 2рЭСФрЭСЮ,
where вДК> 0 is the genus of рЭРґ.
The пђБrst three sections concern the proof of the Natural Embedding theorem
of which most of the details have been included. These sections are based on the
original paper [8] by Korchm√°ros & Torres as well as [15] which is a collection of
notes by Torres and [6], a book by Hirschfeld, Korchm√°ros & Torres. Their work
builds upon work by Garcia & Voloch in [4] from which some of the proofs in
section Section 3.2 originate.
The пђБnal section in this chapter then presents the work by Fanali & Giulietti
in [2] where we use the Natural Embedding theorem to study the case of maximal
curves of Frobenius dimension 3 in detail. The description by Fanali & Giulietti
is then improved upon to obtain a more detailed picture of the curves in ques-
tion. Finally using the description and improvements a computer search has been
performed to completely study the case where рЭСЮ= 4.

3.4
Maximal curves of Frobenius dimension 3
Next we consider maximal curves of Frobenius dimension 3. The case of Frobe-
nius dimension 2 is pretty clear cut as any maximal curve of Frobenius dimen-
sion 2 can be embedded in a Hermitian variety of dimension 1. However, as we
showed in Proposition 2.3.1 this variety is isomorphic to the Hermitian curve and
since рЭЬЛis itself an isomorphism the only curve (up to isomorphism) of Frobe-
nius dimension 2 is the Hermitian curve. Following the approach by Fanali &
Giulietti in [2] we now try to use the Natural Embedding Theorem to say some-
thing meaningful about curves of Frobenius dimension 3.
Suppose for a start
that рЭРњ(рЭРЈ) = span {рЭСУ0, рЭСУ1, рЭСУ2, рЭСУ3}, where the рЭСУрЭСЦhave distinct pole orders in our
пђБxed rational place рЭТЂ0. Take рЭСЗ, рЭСЛ, рЭСМ, рЭСНto be coordinate functions of рЭЬЛ(рЭРґ) with
рЭЬЛ= [рЭСУ0 вИґрЭСУ1 вИґрЭСУ2 вИґрЭСУ3]. Now by a linear transformation if necessary we may assume
our embedding is given by рЭСНрЭСЮ+1 + рЭСМрЭСЮ+1 вИТрЭСЛрЭСЮрЭСЗвИТрЭСЛрЭСЗрЭСЮ. The automorphism group
of a Hermitian variety acts transitively on it as we saw was the case for рЭСЙ2 (we
will use this without proof but the result can be found in [10, ¬І35 pg. 47]) so
without loss of generality we may assume that рЭСГ0 = рЭСГвИЮ= [0 вИґ1 вИґ0 вИґ0]. Now
the tangent plane at рЭСГвИЮis рЭСЗ= 0 which is also the osculating hyperplane and so

On the other hand we need a collision, so either рЭСІor рЭС¶must have a pole of order рЭСЮ
at рЭТЂвИЮ. By symmetry we assume this to be рЭСІ. Finally, let рЭСЪ1 be the пђБrst nonzero
nongap at рЭСГвИЮ, then рЭС£рЭТЂвИЮ(рЭС¶) = вИТрЭСЪ1 since the functions had distinct pole orders.

3.4.
MAXIMAL CURVES OF FROBENIUS DIMENSION 3
57
assuming рЭСЧ0 > 0 with рЭСПвА≤
рЭСЧвИИрЭФљрЭСЮ2. If рЭСЧ0 = 0 we can apply a similar map to obtain
coпђГcient one for the lowest order term as in this case.
Using the form (3.5) it is possible to search for maximal curves of Frobenius
dimension 3 in SageMath. One way to do this is to assume that all the zeroes of рЭС¶
are rational placces and then construct the polynomial рЭЬЙ(рЭС•, рЭСІ) by constructing it
for all possible combinations of points. Now for рЭСЮ= 4, we can have either рЭСЪ1 = 2
or рЭСЪ1 = 3 and in either case the Weierstrass semigroup has 2 missing numbers,
so a maximal curve of Frobenius dimension 3 over рЭФљ16 will have genus 2 and 33
rational places. The number of unique variations of рЭЬЖрЭЬЙ(рЭС•, рЭСІ) is 717601 for рЭСЮ= 4
but by utilising рЭРњ1(рЭС•, рЭСІ) = рЭС•and рЭРњ2(рЭС•, рЭСІ) = рЭС•вИТрЭСРor рЭРњ2(рЭС•, рЭСІ) = рЭС•вИТрЭС§вИТрЭСІ+ 1
that number can be reduced to 7381 unique variations. Running the search (see
code in Appendix A) we пђБnd 21 maximal curves. According to [2] these curves are
pairwise birationally equivalent and among them is the curve
рЭРґвИґрЭСІ5 + рЭС•4 + рЭС•2 = 0.
(3.6)
In total for рЭСЮ= 4 the maximal curves of Frobenius dimension 3 have genus 2, 33
rational places and are birationally equivalent to the curve given by (3.6).
For рЭСЮ= 5 the search is already infeasible as the computations in рЭФљ25 take longer
in SageMath and the number of curves in in the search space explodes to 1787041
despite employing the improvements used for рЭСЮ= 4 to reduce the search space.
==PAGE:68==
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 4
Further work
As we have seen getting a thorough picture of maximal curves of Frobenius di-
mension 3 for рЭСЮ= 4 is very much possible. The logical next step is to extend this
to larger values of рЭСЮ. We saw that for рЭСЮ= 5 the search space was too large for us
to get any usable results, however in their original paper Fanali & Giulietti did
manage to get a complete classiпђБcation for рЭСЮ= 5. Through a computer search in
MAGMA they managed to show that any maximal curve of Frobenius dimension
3 is birationally equivalent to one of the curves
рЭСМ6 = рЭСЛ5 + 2рЭСЛ4 + 3рЭСЛ3 + 4рЭСЛ2 + 3рЭСЛрЭСМ3
or
рЭСМ5 + рЭСМ= рЭСЛ3.
The former has genus 3 and 56 rational places, while the latter has genus 4 and
66 rational places.
With the result by Fanali & Giulietti the maximal curves of Frobenius dimen-
sion 3 are known completely for рЭСЮ< 7. However, extending this to рЭСЮ= 7 is very
much nontrivial. As we have seen the search space of the method we have used
explodes when we increase рЭСЮ.
To be more precise the size of the search space
(assuming only rational zeroes) is something like
рЭСЮ2 вЛЕ|рЭРґ(рЭФљрЭСЮ2)|рЭСЪ1 = рЭТ™(рЭСЮ2 вЛЕ(рЭСЮ2)рЭСЮ) = рЭТ™(рЭСЮ2рЭСЮ+2),
so unfortunately it grows super exponentially. Employing the speedups discussed
earlier this is reduced slightly to
2рЭСЮвЛЕрЭСЮ2 вЛЕ|рЭРґ(рЭФљрЭСЮ2)|рЭСЪ1вИТ2 = рЭТ™(рЭСЮ2рЭСЮвИТ1).
For рЭСЮ= 7 this becomes around 1011, which could feasibly be done on a powerful
supercomputer.
It might also be possible to create further improvements in the style of what we
have done (i.e. constructing automorphisms that reduce the search space). This
would require further study of the automorphism groups of Hermitian varieties,
which we only studied brieпђВy in this project.
